PEEFACE 


In  the  month  of  Julj,  1845,  Mr.  Stuart  discovered  at  Panmure 
the  original  Register  of  the  Bishoprick  of  Brechin,  which  was 
known  to  Dalrymple,  Innes,  and  onr  first  generation  of  anti- 
quaries,  but  had  escaped  the  notice  of  Macfarlane  and  the  later 
collectors  and  transcribers  (except  General  Hutton),  so  that  its 
contents  were  in  a  manner  new  to  the  Scotch  historical  student. 
With  the  consent  of  the  late  Lord  Panmure,  Mr,  Chalmers  re- 
solved  to  print  this  chartulary,  as  a  secoud  contribution  to  the 
Bannat jne  Club,  and  he  devoted  his  leisure  for  several  years  to 
coUecting  materials  to  illustrate  it.  His  collections  were  scarcelj 
completed  at  his  death,  and,  unfortunatelj,  no  connected  notice, 
nor  even  full  notes  of  the  results  of  his  investigations,  have  been 
found  among  his  papers.  A  few  scattered  memoranda  and  notes 
on  the  margins  of  his  books  seem  to  be  all  that  remain  to 
mark  the  progress  of  his  inquiry  ;  and  thus,  it  is  to  be  feared, 
his  country,  in  his  death,  has  lost  the  store  of  information  he 
had  treasured  up  by  assiduous  and  most  intelligent  study.  di- 
rected  chiefly  to  the  illustration  of  his  own  county  and  diocese. 
Even  the  method  and  guiding  principle  of  the  present  ample

light  and  interest.

From   such  various  sources  were  collected  the  documents 


iv  PREFACE. 

which  Mr.  Chalmers  designed  for  the  illustration  of  the  history 
of  his  Diocese  and  its  Cathedral  of  venerable  antiquity. 

Brechiu  was  a  seat  of  ecclesiastical  institutions  long  before 
King  Uavid  founded  its  Bishoprick.  An  ancient  chronicle,  speak- 
ing  of  Kenneth  Mac  Malcolm  (who  is  supposed  to  have  reigned 
about  the  end  of  the  tenth  centurj),  says — Hic  est  qui  tribuit 
magnam  civitatem  Brechne  Domino.^  Here,  certainly,  as  in 
a  few  other  places  of  Scotland,  were  preserved  vestiges  of  that 
primoeval  Christian  estabhshment  which  had  fallen  into  strange 
laxity  of  life,  and,  perhaps,  of  doctrine,  and  was  destined  to  give 
way  altogether  before  the  new  and  stricter  rule  iutroduced  by 
Saint  David.  Among  the  few  architectural  relics  of  that  early 
church,  the  Culdees  of  Brechiu,  like  the  Culdees  of  Abernethy, 
have  left  us  one  of  those  remarkable  "  round  towers"  which 
serve  to  perpetuate  the  memory  of  the  Irish  origin  of  the  church 
which  gave  them  birth. 

We  have  charter  evidence  of  a  college  of  Culdees  at  Brechin 
existing  before,  and  remaining  for  some  time  after,  the  erection 
of  the  Episcopal  see.  There  is  proof,  indeed,  that,  iipon  its 
erection,  the  old  Culdee  convent  and  its  Prior  (submitting,  it 
may  be,  to  stricter  rule  of  disciphne),  became,  as  perhaps  in 
other  cases,  the  electoral  Chapter  of  the  new  Bishoprick.  But 
the  head  of  this  Culdee  convent,  the  Abbot  of  Brechin,  had 
already  become  secularised,  and  had  appropriatcd  to  himself, 
and  transmitted  to  his  family,  the  territories  which  his  pre- 
decessors  had  administered  for  the  church.     During  the  reign 

I  Apud  Innes'  Critical  Essay,  p.  788. 


PREFACE.  V 

of  David  I.  this  "  impropriator "  occurs,  sometiines  stjled 
Leod  of  Brechin,  sometimes  Leod  the  Abbot,  but  still  among  the 
lay  lords  who  accompanied  the  King,  not  ranked  with  the  clerics/ 

In  the  reign  of  William  the  Lion,  we  find  Dovenald,  Abbot 
of  Brechin,  granting  lands  to  the  monks  of  Arbroath,  for  the 
weal  of  his  father  Sampson.  Dovenald  is  elsewhere  styled  the 
grandson  of  Leod ;  so  it  is  probable  that  Sampson  was  son  of 
Leod,  and,  perhaps,  Abbot  of  Brechin.  By  this  time,  the  office 
of  Abbot  had  given  a  sort  of  surname  to  the  family ;  and  they 
still  keep  up  their  connection  with  the  Culdees ;  for  Maolbride, 
the  Prior,  witnesses  the  Abbofsgrant  to  Arbroath  ;'  and  another 
Prior,  Bricius,  as  still  really  a  clerk,  has  precedence  in  a  Bishop's 
charter,  over  Abbot  Dovenald,  now  regarded  as  onhr  a  laynian.-' 

lu  1219  John  was  Abbot  of  Brechin,*  and  one  o).  his  charters 
is  witnessed  by  Morgund  and  John  his  sons,  and  ,Malcolm  his 
brother.^ 

With  Morgund,  who  derived  his  surname  from  the  fami ly 
office,  the  race  of  the  Abbots  of  tho  Culdees  of  Brechin  dis- 
appears.  The  next  personage  who  derived  his  style  from 
Brechin,  was  Henry  de  Brechin,  the  son  of  David  Earl  of  Hunt- 
ingdon,  in  whose  descendants  the  Lordship  of  Brechin  (probably 
identical  with  the  old  lay  Abbacy)  remained  till  the  Black 
Parliament  of  King  Robert  I. 

The  history  of  the  successive  Bishops  of  Brechin  is  very  de-

1.  T.  Bishop,  0.  1155-6. 

2.  Sampon,  c.  1158. 

3.  Turpin,  elect  1178;  was  probably  conseorated  in  that 
year,  and  may  have  been  Bishop  till  1198. 

4.  Ralph  was  elect  in  1198  ;  consecrated  1202. 

5.  His  successor  probably  was  Hugh,  who  styles  Ralph  his 
predecessor,^  and  who  must  have  been  Bishop  of  Brechin  in  1214, 
since  one  of  his  charters  is  witnessed  by  Robert  Elect  of  Ross, 
who  was  so  designated  in  that  year.     Hugh  died  in  1218. 

6.  Gregory  the  archdeacon  succeeded,  who  enumerates  Tur- 
pin,  Ealph,  and  Hugh,  as  his  predecessors  in  the  Bishoprick  of 
Brechin      He  continued  Bishop  till  1246. 

(Gilbert  is  said  to  have  died  Bishop  of  Brechin  in  1249  ;  suc- 
ceeded  by  his  Archdeacon  Robert,  perhaps  through  confusion 
of  a  former  event  in  the  See)  ^

7.  In  1247,  Pope  Innocent  IV.  ordered  tlie  consecration  of 
Albinus,  formerly  Preceutor  (?)  of  the  Cathedral,  who  had  been 
elected  by  the  Chapter,  Bishop  of  Brechin,  dispensing  with  his 
illegitimacj.^  Albinus  enumerates  as  his  predecessors  Turpin, 
Kalph,  Hugh,  and  Gregory.  In  1248  Albinus  witnessed,  and  had 
evidently  promoted,  the  foundation  olthe  Maison  Dieu  by  William 
de  Brechin,  grandsou  of  David,  Earl  of  Huntingdon.  Albinus, 
Bishop  of  Brechin,  by  oharter  dated  at  Finchale,  in  1248,  granted 
an  indulgence  to  all  who  should  help  the  building  of  the  new 
church  of  the  Priory  of  Finchale ;  and  by  a  charter  dated  at 
Durham  4th  March,  1256,  a  similar  indulgeuce  to  those  visiting 
any  of  the  five  altars  in  front  of  the  Church  of  Durham.-  He 
is  known,  also,  from  transactions  with  the  Monks  of  Arbroath, 
and  he  died  Bishop  in  1269.^ 

8.  The  next  steps  are  uncertain  ;  but  a  chronicle,  not  of  the 
highest  authority,^  has  perhaps  given  the  account  most  recon- 
cilable  with  record.  On  the  death  of  i^lbinus,  William  Dean 
of  Brechin,  a  man  of  excellent  character,  but  of  a  harsh  voice, 
was  elected  by  the  Chapter.  The  Legate  refused  him  confirma- 
tion,  but  on  appeal  the  Pope  at  once  granted  it.  William,  elect 
of  Brechin,  died  in  1274  in  the  Council  at  Lyons,  and  Friar 
William,  of  the  Friars  Preachers  (  Rector  fratrum  predicatorum 
de  Perth),*  was  elected  Bishop  of  Brechin,  who  refused  the  See 
on  account  of  the  smallness  of  the  revenues.

(Keith,  quoting  the  Chartularj  of  Arbroath,  which  he  pro- 
bably  had  not  himself  seen,  states  that  Robert,  formerly  Arch- 
deacon  of  this  See,  was  Bishop  thereof  in  the  year  1284.) 

9.  Williara,  Bishop  of  Brechin,  granted  an  indulgence  at 
Durham  on  16th  August,  1286;  and  William  was  Bishop  in 
1290. ' 

10.  John  de  Kininmunth  was  Bishop  of  Brechin  on  21 
October,  1304.  He  was  one  of  the  Bishops,  who,  in  1309,  re- 
cognised  Robert  Bruce's  title  to  the  crown.  He  is  named  as 
Bishop  in  1313,  1318,  1321,  and  in  the  sixteenth  year  of  the 
reign  of  Robert  (1323).     We  do  not  learn  when  he  died. 

11.  The  next  Bishop  was  Adam  de  Moravia,  who  filled  the 
See  frorn  1328  to  1349.  Bishop  Adam  is  mentioned  by  name 
in  the  passports  for  the  Ambassadors  to  England  in  1 336,  and 
again,  among  the  Ambassadors  treating  of  the  ransom  of 
David  II.,  in  1347-8-9."  By  a  clerical  error  in  our  Eegister, 
a  precept  of  David  II.,  in  the  thirty  -  first  of  his  reign 
(1359)  is  made  to  be  directed  to  Adam,  instead  of  Patrick, 
Bishop  of  Brechin,  "  Chancellor ;"  and  on  that  authority  Spottis- 
wood  has  erroneously  stated  that  Adam  was  chancellor  of  Scot- 
land. 

12.  Philip  was  Bishop  of  Brechin  on  the  13th  March, 
1350.^ 

13.  Patrick  de  Locrys,  previously  Rector  of  Tyningham,  be- 
came  Bishop  of  Brechin  in  1354,  and,  about  the  same  time,

Chancelloi-  of  the  Kingdom.  He  was  Bishop  and  Chancellor 
from  1358  to  8  Uocember,  1368  ;  but  was  Bishop  and  no  longer 
Chancellor  on  4th  April,  40th  David  II.  (1369.)^  On  the  mor-

stitution  of  the  Chapter  of  his  Cathedral,-  and  he  was  Bishop, 
and  present  in  Parliaraent  on  the  4th  April,  1373. 

14.  Stephen  was  Bishop  of  Brechin  on  the  23d  February, 
1384,^  when  he  erected  the  Church  of  Lethnot  into  a  prebend 
of  the  Cathedral. 

15.  Walter  Forestar,  Canon  of  Aberdeen,  Secretarj  to  the 
King,  and  Lord  Clerk  Register,  got  the  Bishoprick  of  Brechin 
as  early  as  1401.  On  16th  Julj,  1420,^  he  assisted  at  a  pro- 
vincial  synod  and  council  general  of  the  clergy  of  the  kingdom 
held  at  Perth.  In  1423  and  1424,  Patrick,  Bishop  of  Brechin, 
had  passports  from  the  King  of  England  for  returning  from 
Flanders.^ 

16.  John  de  Crannoch  was  Bishop  of  Brechin  on  Trinity 
Sunday  the  22d  day  of  May,  1429,  when  "  Walter  Stewart, 
knight,  Palatyne  of  Stratherne,  of  Athole  and  of  Cathenes  erle, 
lorde  of  Brechyn  and  Cortequhy"  made  the  munificent  gift  to  the 
Church  of  an  annual  rent  of  £40  out  of  the  lands  of  Corto- 
quhy."  Bishop  John  seems  to  have  gone  on  more  than  one 
embassy  to  France  for  James  L,  besides  accompanying  the 
Scotch  princess  to  her  ill-omened  marriage  with  the  Dauphin.

X  PREFACE. 

On  14  April,  1434,  in  a  general  chapter  held  in  the  chapter 
house  at  Brechin,  being  the  morrow  after  a  Synod  had  sat, 
Bishop  John  narrated  how  upon  the  sea,  in  his  last  voyage  from 
France,  he  had  made  a  vow  to  give  to  the  Church  of  Brechin,  in 
houour  of  the  Blessed  Trinity,  two  silver  candlesticks,  weighing 
nine  marks  Troy,  in  acquittance  of  which  vow  he  delivered  to 
John  Liall,  the  Treasurer  of  the  Church,  six  silver  cups,  gilt  ou 
the  edges,  of  the  weight  of  nine  marks  Troy,  in  one  case  of  red 
leather.  Moreover,  the  Bishop  delivered  into  the  hands  of  the 
Treasurer  a  silver  gilt  cup  and  cover,  of  the  weight  of  four  marks 
Troy,  the  cover  having  the  rays  of  the  sun  spread  over  it,  in  a 
leather  case,  to  be  preserved  and  kept  in  the  treasury  for  the  use 
of  the  Dean  and  Canons  in  their  common  symposia,  and  for  no 
other  use.^  It  is  another  scene  of  church  life  we  find  recorded 
in  an  entry  of  our  Register  on  the  20th  and  21st  of  August  1448. 
Bishop  John  is  at  war  with  the  Dean  about  the  tax  that  ought 
to  have  been  levied  upon  the  Canons,  under  the  name  of  repair- 
ing  the  hoods  of  the  choir.  He  fulminates  excommunication 
against  the  Archdeacon  for  laying  violent  hands  on  his  Lordship, 
and  waxing  still  more  wrath,  admonishes  the  Dean,  then  present, 
to  remove  from  his  house  his  concubine,  and  to  abstain  from  all 
cohabitation,  or  suspect  conversation  with  her,  under  pain  of 
£40  current  Scotch  mouey.  John,  Bishop  of  Brechin,  was 
Conservator  of  the  privileges  of  the  Scotican  Church  in  1445  ; 
and  John  was  still  Bishop    in    1450,    and    on   4    September,

rick  from  1693  to  1701 — the  only  instance  of  such  promotion 
after  the  abolition  of  Episcopacy  in  Scotland.

The  Cathedral  of  Brechin  was  dedicated  to  the  Holy  Trinity. 
When  we  first  become  acquainted  with  the  individuals  compos- 
ing  the  Chapter,  the  Culdees  had  long  disappeared.  Not  even 
their  name  was  preserved.  The  constitution  of  the  Chapter  was, 
however,  though  entirelj  secular,  somewhat  peculiar,  and  cannot 
be  better  described  than  in  the  words  of  the  '  Declaratio,'  1372, 
which  aifords  a  remarkable  contrast  to  the  usual  verbosity  of 
snch  records.  We  find,  says  the  Bishop  and  Ex-Chancellor  of 
4^cotland,  that  there  are  eleven  benefices  of  old  erected  into 
Canonries  in  the  Cathedral — of  which  four  are  Dignities — 
namely,  those  of  the  Dean,  Precentor,^  Chancellor,  and  Trea- 
surer;  the  fifth,  the  ofiice  of  the  Archdeacon' — all  five  incom- 
patible  with  other  benefices.  The  other  six  benefices — those, 
nainely,  of  the  Vicar,  the  Pensioner,  the  Sub-Dean,  with  the 
prebends  of  Kilmoir,  Butirgill,  and  Guthrie,  we  find  to  be  sim- 
ple  prebends,  and  compatible  with  any  other  benefice,  even  with 
cure ;  and  it  is  expressly  declared  that  albeit  two  of  the  pre-

Earl  of  Crawford. 

The  Bishop  sat  in  the  Chapter  as  Rector  of  Brechin,  that 
being  his  prebend.  The  Vicarius  was,  perhaps,  so  named  from 
acting  as  the  choral  vicar  of  the  Bishop.  The  Pensionarius  had 
an  anuual  rent  from  land,  or  pension,  instead  of  church  and 
tithes.

xviii  PREFACE. 

otber  things,  the  frequent  and  gradually  increasing  interference 
of  the  Popes  with  the  patronage  of  all  ecclesiastical  benefices. 
The  considerations  which  weighed  at  Rome  are  carefully  set 
forth  in  the  transaction  with  Guaspar  Bonciani,  a  Florentine 
inerchant  and  the  correspondent  of  the  great  Roman  bankers, 
Philip  de  Strozzi  &  Co.,  for  procuring  the  promotion  of  William 
Meldrum  to  the  Bishoprick  in  1488.^ 

The  dilapidation  of  church  property  upon  the  eve  of  the  Re- 
formation  is  more  marked  in  Brechin  than  other  dioceses.  The 
Earl  of  Argyll  procured  his  kinsman,  Alexander  Campbell,  while 
yet  a  child,  to  be  appointed  to  the  Bishopric,  with  express  power 
of  "  giving  and  disposing  all  the  benefices  which  previously 
were  in  the  Bishop's  gift."  How  the  boy  Bishop  was  made  to 
use  that  power  is  seen  in  the  large  alienations  executed  in  1566-7, 
apparently  even  before  he  had  begun  the  cducation  which  we 
find  him  carrying  ou  "  in  Geneva  at  the  schuillis"  in  1573. 

The  other  charters  of  the  period  of  the  Reformation,  while 
marking  the  ra[)id  dilapidation  of  the  Church  property,  are  of 
great  interest  to  the  local  antiquary,  and  furnish  the  chief  means 
for  tracing  the  history  of  the  Episcopal  city  and  its  buildings. 
Unfortunately,  the  Round  Tower  of  mysterious  antiquity  is  not 
once  mentioned.  The  anti-reformation  clergy  treated  this  soli- 
tary  monument  of  the  primseval  church  with  the  same  neglect 
as  their  successors.^

One  deed,  the  grant  of  a  toft  and  croft  in  Kiticardin/  with 
liberty  of  hrewing  and  baking  and  of  all  else  pertaining  to 
a  free  hostellry,  recalls  the  curious  tenures  that  sometimes 
gathered  round  our  Royal  dwellings  The  Castle  of  Kincardin, 
an  abode  of  our  earlier  monarchs,  was  surrounded  by  little  tene- 
ments  appropriated  for  the  dwelling  and  support  of  the  retainers 
of  the  court ;  and  many  of  these  retained  the  names  of  the  officers 
whose  appanage  they  were.'-  Another  deed,  a  C  rown  charter 
of  the  Hermitage  of  Kilgerry,"  opens  a  humbler  piece  of  Angus 
local  history.  Hugh  Cuminche  was  the  Herrnit,  and  possessed 
the  hermitage  of  St.  Mary's  Chapel  of  the  Forest  of  Kilgerry 
in  the  barony  of  Menmuir,  hy  a  heredita  y  title,  together  with 
the  croft,  the  green,  and  three  acres  of  arable  land.  These  he 
resigned  in  the  hands  of  the  Sovereign,  who  regranted  them  to 
John  Smyth,  citizen  of  Brechin,  on  12  May,  1445,  for  payment 
of  services  used  and  wont,  and  with  a  special  reservation  of  the 
accustomed  benefit  of  the  Hermifs  prayers.  It  does  not  appear 
that  John  Smyth  was  qualified  to  perform  this  part  of  the  Red- 
dendo,  or  to  serve  in  the  Forest  Chapel  of  the  Virgin ;  the  ruins 
of  which,  till  lately,  existed  in  the  vicinity  of  a  fine  spring,  still 
known  as  the  Lady  Well.^     The  Brechin  citizen  soldthe  hermit-

The  late  Patrick  Chalmers  was  born  at  Aldbar,  on  the  81st  Oc- 
tober,  1802.  His  father,  though  inheriting  a  good  Scotch  property, 
was  engaged  in  extensive  trade  as  a  Loiidon  merchant,  and  lived 
chieflynear  London  till  1819,  when  he  cametoresidepermanently 
at  Aldbar,  where  he  died  in  1826.  Previous  to  1819  Patrick 
was  little  in  Scotland.  He  was  edacated  partly  at  home  and 
partly  at  the  pri vate  school  of  Dr.  Coghan,  a  good  scholar  and  par- 
ticularly  esteemed  a  good  Grecian,  at  Higham,  two  miles  fromhis 
father's  house  at  Walthamstow,  a  neighbourhood  that  permitted 
his  spending  his  Saturdays  and  Sundays  at  home  Though 
Chalmers  was  long  remembered  by  Dr.  Coghan  as  a  promising 
pupil,  he  brought  away  from  school  a  very  slender  amount  of 
classical  scholarship,  and  hardly  increasedit  during  his  residence  at

Oxford.  He  was  entered  at  Queen's  College  wheu  sixteen,  but 
did  not  reside  long  enough  to  take  a  degree  ;  and  was  gazetted 
to  a  cornetcy  in  the  Third  Dragoon  Guards  in  the  autumn  of 
1821.  Most  of  his  military  life  was  spent  in  ireland.  He  at- 
tained  the  rank  of  Captain,  but  retired  from  service  shortly  after 
his  father's  death,  and  then  settled  at  Aldbar. 

Chalmers's  first  phasis  of  country  life  was  as  a  sports- 
man.  He  kept  a  pack  of  fox-hounds,  and  after  the  first  season 
was  his  own  huntsman — an  oflBce  which  he  filled  with  good  re- 
pute,  but  which  subjected  him  to  fatigue  and  exposure,  that  pro- 
bably  had  an  injurious  efi^ect  on  his  constitution.  The  death 
of  a  young  lady  to  whom  he  was  engaged,  in  1830,  affccted  him 
deeply,  and  this  shock,  together  with  an  attack  of  ilhiess,  led 
him  to  give  up  his  hounds.  The  country  was  now  agitated  with 
the  great  question  of  Parliamentary  Reform  ;  and  Chalmers,  long 
ere  this  thoroughly  liberal  in  politics,  threw  himself  into  the 
struggle  with  the  same  zeal  and  energy  he  had  shown  as  a  sports- 
man.  After  a  gallant  but  unsuccessful  contest  in  1832,'  he  was 
elected  in  183*  without  opposition,  as  Member  for  the  Mon- 
trose  District  of  burghs,  and  was  again  returned  for  the  same 
constituency  at  the  next  general  election.  As  soon  as  he  had 
taken  his  seat  in  the  House,  he  devoted  himself  to  the  study  of

PREFAGE.  XXV 

its  history  and  forms,  his  duty  in  Parliament  and  in  committees, 
and  to  the  local  interests  of  his  constituents,  with  indefatigable 
industry,  and  with  an  honesty  of  purpose  which  his  stoutest  op- 
ponents  never  questioned.  There  was  no  local  public  improve- 
ment  in  which  he  did  not  bear  a  leading  part ;  and  his  cousti- 
tuents  appreciated  the  enlightened  interest  he  took  in  all  their 
afFairs.  His  services  to  the  Burghshe  represented  were  acknow- 
ledged  by  repeated  public  expressions  of  their  approbation.  It 
was  not  forgotten  that  he  maintained  the  principles  of  freedom 
before  they  had  become  fashionable ;  and  the  feeling  of  regret 
when  failing  health  compelled  him  to  retire  from  Farliament 
was  deep  and  universal. 

In  July,  1839,  Chalmers  married  Jessie  Anna  Letitia,  the 
younger  of  two  daughters  of  John  Herbert  Foley  of  Ridgway, 
Pembrokeshire,  and  widow  of  Thomas  Taylor  Veruon  of  Han- 
bury  Hall,  Worcestershire.  In  less  than  a  year,  on  t'.\e  7th 
April,  1840,  that  lady  died,  leaving  two  sons  by  her  first  hus- 
band.  In  addition  to  the  sorrow  of  so  untimely  a  bereavement, 
Chalmers's  parliamentary  attendauce  was  now  fi-equently  in- 
terrupted  by  attacks  of  illness,  and  a  chronic  malady  which 
never  afterwards  entirely  quitted  him.  About  the  close  of  the 
session  of  1841,  he  was  advised  to  retire  to  Aldbar,  and  to  seek, 
in  complete  repose,  a  cure  for  a  threatening  of  spine  disease  ; 
and,  in  spring  of  1842,  he  gave  r<p  his  seat  in  the  House  of  Com- 
mons,  and  his  active  political  life  ;  though  he  never  ceased  to 
feel  and  express  the  heartiest  interest  on  political  questions,  and 
was  always  ready  with  advice  and  sympathy,  as  well  as  more 


xxvi  PREFACE. 

material  assistance,  for  any  measure  tending  to  the  general  good, 
or  the  interest  of  his  district. 

For  the  next  nine  years  Chalmers  was  constantly  in  ill 
health.  Riding  and  walking  were  alike  impossible  for  him. 
Even  in  summer  he  could  onlj  at  times  take  the  air  in  a  carriage. 
In  winter,  he  was  entirely  confined  to  the  house.  Often  in 
pain,  always  under  the  depression  of  a  wearing  malady,  cut 
off  from  society  as  well  as  from  all  the  pursuits  that  at  different 
times  had  occupied  him,  he  had  to  seek  for  new  employment  for 
his  active  mind,  suited  to  his  altered  circumstances.  He  found 
it  in  the  study  of  the  charter  history  and  antiquities  of  his  native 
county  ;  and  he  bestowed  on  that  study  the  same  energy  he  had 
thrown  into  every  pursuit,  and  tlie  most  admirable  and  perse- 
vering  industry. 

He  had  to  work  from  the  foundation  —  to  recover  his 
school  Latin,  and  to  add  to  it  the  Latin  of  the  middle  ages — 
to  learn  the  handwriting  of  old  charters,  and  the  more  cramp 
hand  of  the  sixteenth  and  seventeenth  centuries  —  to  pick 
up  enough  of  Law  to  understand  the  terms  of  a  tenure — to  study 
the  constitution  of  old  Scotland  and  that  of  the  ancient  Church, 
which  is  at  the  root  of  historical  antiquities— to  be  ableto  apply 
the  criteria  for  dates.  His  first  difficulty,  as  with  all  self-educated 
scholars,  was  to  find  the  apparatus  for  work — sources  of  informa- 
tion.  When  he  began  the  study,  his  library  consisted  mostly  of 
his  grandfather's  books,  who  had  been  a  lawyer  and  Sheriffof  the 
county.  There  was  plenty  of  old  Scotch  Law,  and  a  good  deal 
of  general  literature,  but  of  the  peculiar  books  of  his  new  study, 


PREFACE.  xxvii 

hardlj  anj.  lle  had  to  furnish  himself  with  the  dictionaries  and 
grammars  of  a  new  language  and  science,  and  that  at  a  distance 
from  advice,  as  well  as  from  booksellers'  shops.  But  these  diffi- 
culties  were,  with  Chalmers,  only  incitements  to  persevere.  In  a 
short  time  he  gathered  round  him  a  capital  archfeological  library, 
to  which  he  was  continuallj  adding,  and  which  in  no  long  time 
he  knew  well  how  to  turn  to  use.  He  corresponded,  and  be- 
came  personally  acquainted  with  most  students  of  his  country 
engaged  in  similar  pursuits,  and  his  acquaintances  soon  became 
friends.  He  stood  very  well  with  his  country  neighbours,  and  he 
used  his  popularity  for  obtaining  access  to  every  charter-room  in 
his  county,  and  made  notes  of  all.  His  house  became  the  plea- 
sant  haunt  of  many  an  antiquary  and  scholar,  giving  and  taking 
knowledge.  This  was  so,  even  when  his  thread  of  life  seemed 
worn  to  a  fibre ;  but,  before  the  end  of  the  nine  years  of  habitual 
sickness,  though  still  restricted  to  his  own  house,  he  had,  in  some 
measure,  rscovered  strength  and  the  power  of  locomotion,  and 
was  able  to  enjoy  a  more  general  intercourse. 

Aldbar  was  then  the  centre  of  a  very  cultivated  society.  Along 
with  country  neighbours  and  English  friends  were  to  be  found 
Scotchmen  of  his  own  pursuits,  foreign  antiquaries,  artists,  and 
scholars.  All  men  of  education  or  accomplishment  were  welcome : 
for  he  could  appreciate  intellect,  however  directed,  and  merit  in 
any  dress.  His  reputation  as  a  loca!  antiquary  brought  round  him 
and  imbued  with  liis  tastes,  neighbours  who  had  never  dreamt  of 
antiquities  before, — the  Laii-d  curious  about  a  family  picture  or 
pedigree,  the  Parson  desirous  of  information  as  to  tein'l  or  glebe. 


xxviii  PREFACE. 

His  stores  of  information  were  open  alike  to  alL  If  he  could 
not  solve  the  difficulty,  he  could  advise  where  to  seek  for  its  so- 
lution.  His  own  labour  was  given  freely,  and  his  large  collec- 
tions  and  books  of  reference  were  as  much  for  his  friends  as  for 
his  own  use.  He  devoted  a  good  deal  of  time  to  preparing 
two  works  as  contributions  to  the  Baunatyne  Club — one,  a 
magnificent  collection  of  the  Ancient  Sculptured  Stones  of 
Angus,  those  mouuments  full  of  mysterious  symbols,  and  of  un- 
known  antiquity— the  other,  the  present  Register.  He  pssisted 
largely  in  editing  the  Chartulary  of  Arbroath,  and  contributed 
smaller  essays  to  various  Antiquarian  Societies  and  Repertories. 
Through  all  these  occupations  he  kept  up  a  very  extensive 
correspondence  with  fellow-workmen,  and  knew  whatever  was 
drawing  the  attention  of  Antiquaries  in  all  parts  of  the  world. 

Even  before  he  had  turned  his  mind  to  these  studies,  he  had 
found  occupation  for  hours  of  sickness  in  the  improvement  of  his 
family  estate.  He  was  proud  of  his  picturesque  old  castle,  the 
structure  of  Sir  Thomas  Lyon  of  the  sixteenth  century,  and  de- 
lighted  in  embellishing  it,  and  the  quaint  garden  with  which 
he  surrounded  it.  He  was  a  liberal  and  considerate  landlord, 
and  an  enterprising  improver,  regarding  his  tenants'  advantage 
as  his  own.  For  his  greater  operations  he  chose  times  of  rural 
stagnation,  when  his  employment  was  a  boon  to  the  labourer. 
He  established  schools,  promoted  cleanliness  and  comfort  among 
his  poor  neighbours  ;  and  during  his  long  illness  he  never  ceased 
to  take  the  most  lively  interest  in  county  business,  None  of 
these  objects  were  overshadowed  by  his  literary  pursuits  ;  on  the 


PREFACE.  xxix 

coutrary,  he  pursued  them  with  a  luore  intelligeut  taste,  aud  ou 
higher  principles. 

At  length,  after  niue  years  of  sickness  aud  seclusiou, 
Chalmers's  constitution  rallied,  and  he  was  gradually  restored 
to  comparative  health.  This  naturally  produced  a  chauge  of 
his  habits,  He  gave  somewhat  less  of  his  thoughts  to  anti- 
quariau  studies  and  literature,  aud  devoted  more  time  to  public 
and  county  busiuess,  Amoug  other  objects  of  more  immediate 
interest,  he  applied  his  sound  intellect  and  the  business  habits  he 
liad  acquired  in  bis  parliamentarj  life,  to  working  out  a  system 
of  road  reform  ;  on  which  subject  he  prepared  a  valuable  report 
as  Convener  of  a  committee  of  the  Couuty  appointed  to  cousider 
the  cousolidation  of  road  districts,  in  1851.  After  that  year  his 
health  steadily  improved,  and  admitted  of  his  occasioually  leaving 
home,  and  of  visits  to  Edinburgh  aud  London  every  season.  It 
was  delightful  to  witness  his  enjoymeut  iu  returuing  into 
society,  and  in  using  the  sources  of  iuformation  he  had  so  long 
wished  to  reach.  There  appeared  iu  him  so  much  vigour  and 
freshness  that  his  friends  iudulged  in  the  hope  of  his  entire 
recovery  and  long  life.  It  was  not,  however,  without  misgiviucr 
that  they  heard  him  announce  his  intention  of  making  a  tour  on  the 
continent,  in  compauy  with  his  step-son,  in  the  Spriug  of  1854. 

Chalmers  left  Eugland  iu  high  spirits  ;  and  his  travels  through 
France  and  Italj  gave  him  great  pleasure.  To  compare  the  condi- 
tion  of  the  people,  their  agriculture  and  habits  of  life,  interested 
him  no  less  than  the  study  of  their  history  and  autiquities.  The 
route  of  the  travellers  was  down  the  Rhone  to  its  mouth,  and 


XXX 


PREFACE. 


by  steam  to  Genoa,  Leghorn,  and  Rome.  On  their  arrival  at 
Rome,  in  the  middle  of  March,  Chahiiers  was  attacked  by  small- 
pox,  but  made  a  rapid  recovery,  and  spent  some  time  in  diligent 
study  of  the  language,  as  well  as  the  antiquities  of  the  city,  so 
interesting  to  an  antiquary. 

In  May,  he  w^ent  to  Naples,  and,  while  there,  had  some 
alarming  symptoms  of  his  old  malady.  He  was  advised  to  leave 
Naples,  and  imprudently  undertook  the  journey  back  to  Rome 
by  land.  His  complaint  was  aggravated  by  the  exertion,  and  he 
arrived  in  a  state  of  great  exhaustion,  from  which  he  never  re- 
covered.  He  survived  only  a  few  days,  and  died  at  Rome,  on 
the  23d  of  June,  1854.  His  remains  were  brought  home,  and 
the  body  of  Patrick  Chalmers  is  buried  beside  his  father  and 
mother  in  the  cemetery  of  the  little  chapel  in  the  glen,  which 
was  of  old  the  parish  church  of  Aldbar,  and  which  he  had  taken 
great  pleasure  in  restoring  and  rebuilding,  chiefly  with  the 
stones  of  the  ruined  church. 

His  untimely  death  is  deplored  by  the  antiquaries  and 
scholars  of  Scotlaud,  and  not  by  them  only.  If  there  be  any- 
where  a  belief  that  the  cultivation  of  such  studies  as  his,  unfits 
a  man  for  tho  real  business  of  life,  his  course  of  energetic  and 
practical  usefuhiess  should  go  far  to  remove  it.  In  the  circle  of 
his  own  connections,  among  his  friends  and  in  society,  he  was 
much  beloved.  He  was  an  exemplary  landlord  and  country 
gentleman,  while  he  did  something  to  raise  the  standard  of  re- 
finement  among  the  rural  aristocracy.  As  a  politician,  an 
economist  and  statist,  a  promoter  of   social  improvement,  he 


PREFACE. 


XXXI 


held  a  higli  place.  Through  life  he  was  an  earnest  supporter 
of  those  principles  which,  though  they  now  form  part  of  our  con- 
stitution,  had  few  advocates  among  country  gentlemen  when  he 
began  his  career.  He  was  a  zealous  friend  to  the  cause  of  edu- 
cation,  yet  entirely  free  from  sectarian  bias.  To  every  public 
and  patriotic  movement  within  the  sphere  of  his  influence  his 
support  was  given  with  unfailing  constancy.  In  his  own  district 
his  death  was  felt  to  be  a  great  public  calamity,  and  all  who 
take  an  interest  in  his  native  county  of  Angus  will  long  lament 
the  loss  of  Patrick  Chalmers. 

C.  INNES.

et  cum  omnibus  forinsecis  sectis  et  aliis  que  ad  dictum  molendinum 
pertinent  et  poterunt  pertinere.  Et  si  contingat  quod  dicte  terre  vel 
aliqua  dictarum  terrarum  per  rae  vel  per  aliquem  heredum  vel 
successorum  meorum  quoquo  modo  impei-petuum  vel  ad  tempus  alien- 
entur  quod  semper  multura  dictarum  terrarum  secundum  mensuras  prius 
nominatas  penes  dictam  domum  sine  aliqua  diminucione  permaneat. 
Volo  eciam  et  concedo  pro  me  et  heredibus  [meis]  quod  dictum  molendi- 
num  et  stagnum  eiusdem  per  homines  de  schira  sustententur  et  impos- 


6        REGISTRUM  EPISCOPATUS  BRECHINENSIS. 

terum  reparentur  sicut  hactenus  dum  dictum  molendinum  fuit  in  manu 
mea  consueuit  fieri.  Molendinarii  vero  dicti  molendini  per  magistrum 
dicte  domus  et  non  per  alium  institui  et  destitui  debent.  Volo  eciam 
quod  dicti  magister  et  homines  existentes  in  dicta  domo  et  omnes 
homines  in  eorum  terris  habitantes  a  reparacione  dictorum  stagni  et 
molendini  sintimmunes.  Volo  insuper  quod  ubi  terre  dicte  domus  sunt 
contigue  adiacentes  terris  meis  quod  ab  eorum  terris  habeant  commu- 
nem  pasturam  inter  terram  meam  eisdem  adiacentem.  Tenendam  et 
habendam  piedicte  domui  magistro  capellanis  ac  pauperibus  eiusdem 
domus  imperpetuum  cum  oranibus  asiamentis  ad  villam  de  Brechine 
spectantibus  in  moris  et  maresiis  in  pratis  pascuis  et  coraraunibus 
pasturis  in  petariis  et  in  omnibus  aliis  libertatibus  et  asiamentis  inter 
terram  raeam  de  Brechine  existentibus  tam  pro  seipsis  quara  pro 
horainibus  suis  habitantibus  infra  diuisas  predictas  adeo  libere  quiete 
plenarie  et  honorifice  sicut  aliqua  elimosina  in  regno  Scocie  a  comite 
vel  barone  in  puram  et  perpetuam  elimosinam  data  et  concessa 
liberius  quiecius  plenius  et  honorificencius  tenetur  et  possidetur.  Nec 
volo  quod  aliquod  soluunt  racione  predictorum  nisi  tantum  preces  et 
oraciones  debitas  et  deuotas  et  ego  Willelmus  et  heredes  mei  pre- 
dictam  terram  totam  et  predictum  molendinum  cura  oranibus  asiamen- 
tis  et  pertinenciis  suis  prout  superius  plenius  continetur  predicte 
domui  et  magistro  capellanis  et  pauperibus  contra  oranes  homines  et 
feminas  varantizabimus  imperpetuura  acquietabimus  et  defendemus 
tam  in  forinsecis  quam  in  aliis.  Et  sciendum  quod  cum  magister  illius 
doraus  decesserit  vel  decesserint  alius  loco  sui  per  me  heredes  meos  et 
non  per  alium  episcopo  loci  qui  pro  tempore  fuerit  debet  presentari 
et  ad  nostram  presentacionem  ibidem  institui.  Remanente  eidem  epis- 
copo  tantummodo  cura  spiritualium  et  obseruato  michi  et  heredibus 
meis  tantuni  jure  patronatus.     Et  magister  dicte   domus  qui  ad  meam

presentacionem  vel  heredum  meorum  in  eadem  fuerit  institutus  liabe-
